"""Top-k for RTX 3090 (SM86), shape-adaptive.

The reference uses a multi-pass radix select (~8 passes for fp32). These shapes
are tiny (each row is 16 KB - 512 KB), so per-call time is dominated by kernel
work, not DRAM. Two kernels cover the deck, picked per (k, n):

  * Small k (1, 8, 16) -- per-row register cascade. One block per row; each
    thread streams a strided slice keeping a sorted-descending register buffer
    of size K (the K largest it has seen). Buffers are merged across the block
    by a log2(BLOCK)-deep merge-tree in shared mem (stride K+1 to dodge 32-way
    bank conflicts). All-register, static-index cascade (a dynamic index into a
    register array spills to local memory; a shared-mem min-heap was tried and
    is slower due to shmem load latency in the dependent sift-down).

  * Large k (32, 64) -- per-row radix-256 histogram select. Four byte-passes
    (MSB first) build the monotonic uint of the k-th largest via a 256-bin
    histogram, then gather + in-shmem bitonic sort. ~12 barriers total vs ~96
    for a 1-bit radix, and 4x fewer row reads -- far cheaper than the O(n*K)
    cascade once K is large.

  * The single very-large row (n=131072, k=64) -- segmented radix: many blocks
    each radix-select their contiguous segment to top-K into a temp buffer,
    then one block radix-selects the merged candidates. Keeps the whole GPU
    busy instead of one block.

Correctness: the per-thread cascade buffer is size K (any single thread sees
at most K of the global top-K, since only K elements are >= the k-th value), so
the global top-K survives to the merge. Radix finds the exact k-th largest's
monotonic uint (the largest value with count(>=v) >= k), so the gather collects
exactly the top-K (ties filled to K). Both paths return values sorted
descending; indices are checked leniently by the harness (gather-vs-reference).

Build: the CUDA is compiled to a standalone .so (extern "C", called via ctypes)
so it never pulls in the ML framework's C++ headers -- the installed framework's
List_inl.h is ill-formed under gcc>=14 and would fail an extension build.
"""
from __future__ import annotations

import ctypes
import os
import subprocess

import torch
import torch.nn as nn

OP_TYPE = "topk"
SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"]
HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_3090"]

_SEG_THRESHOLD = 1 << 15  # 32768; above this, use multi-block segmented path

_CUDA_SRC = r"""
#include <cuda_runtime.h>

// Per-thread: insert v (global index gi) into a sorted-DESCENDING register
// buffer tv[0..K-1] (tv[0]=largest, tv[K-1]=smallest=threshold). Drops old min.
// Fully-unrolled conditional-cascade with STATIC indices only -- dynamic
// indexing into a register array would spill it to local memory (very slow).
// O(K) per qualifying element, but all-register so latency is 1 cycle/step;
// a shmem min-heap (O(log K)) was tried and is slower due to shmem load
// latency in the data-dependent sift-down chain.
template <int K>
__device__ __forceinline__ void insert_topk(float* tv, int* ti, float v, int gi) {
    if (v <= tv[K - 1]) return;
    float carry = v; int carry_i = gi; bool active = false;
    #pragma unroll
    for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) {
        if (carry > tv[i]) active = true;
        if (active) {
            float tf = tv[i]; tv[i] = carry; carry = tf;
            int t2 = ti[i]; ti[i] = carry_i; carry_i = t2;
        }
    }
}

// Compile-time bitonic sort of a size-K register buffer, DESCENDING. Static
// indices only -> no register spill.
template <int K>
__device__ __forceinline__ void sort_desc(float* tv, int* ti) {
    #pragma unroll
    for (int size = 2; size <= K; size <<= 1)
    #pragma unroll
    for (int stride = size >> 1; stride > 0; stride >>= 1)
    #pragma unroll
    for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) {
        int j = i ^ stride;
        if (j > i) {
            bool up = ((i & size) == 0);
            if ((up && tv[i] < tv[j]) || (!up && tv[i] > tv[j])) {
                float f = tv[i]; tv[i] = tv[j]; tv[j] = f;
                int g = ti[i]; ti[i] = ti[j]; ti[j] = g;
            }
        }
    }
}

// Merge-tree reduction over `nbuf` shared-mem buffers (each size K, sorted
// desc, laid out at stride S=K+1 to dodge 32-way bank conflicts). After return,
// buffer 0 holds the top-K of the union, sorted desc.
// Pairs merged at distance d=1,2,4,...; one __syncthreads per level.
template <int BLOCK, int K>
__device__ __forceinline__ void merge_tree(float* sv, int* si, int nbuf) {
    constexpr int S = K + 1;   // coprime to 32 -> no bank conflicts
    for (int d = 1; d < nbuf; d <<= 1) {
        for (int base = threadIdx.x * (2 * d); base + d < nbuf;
             base += BLOCK * (2 * d)) {
            float* A = sv + base * S; int* Ai = si + base * S;
            float* B = sv + (base + d) * S; int* Bi = si + (base + d) * S;
            float tmp[K]; int ti2[K];
            int ia = 0, ib = 0;
            #pragma unroll
            for (int c = 0; c < K; c++) {
                float va = A[ia], vb = B[ib];
                if (va >= vb) { tmp[c] = va; ti2[c] = Ai[ia]; ia++; }
                else         { tmp[c] = vb; ti2[c] = Bi[ib]; ib++; }
            }
            #pragma unroll
            for (int c = 0; c < K; c++) { A[c] = tmp[c]; Ai[c] = ti2[c]; }
        }
        __syncthreads();
    }
}

template <int K, int BLOCK>
__global__ void topk_reduce_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out_vals,
    long long* __restrict__ out_idx, float* __restrict__ tmp_vals,
    long long* __restrict__ tmp_idx, int batch, int n, int nseg, int seg_size)
{
    const int row = blockIdx.x;
    const int seg = blockIdx.y;
    if (row >= batch) return;
    const int seg_start = seg * seg_size;
    const int seg_end = (seg_start + seg_size < n) ? (seg_start + seg_size) : n;
    const float* xrow = x + (size_t)row * n;

    constexpr int M = BLOCK * (K + 1);
    extern __shared__ float smem[];
    float* sv = smem;
    int* si = reinterpret_cast<int*>(sv + M);

    float tv[K]; int ti[K];
    #pragma unroll
    for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { tv[i] = -INFINITY; ti[i] = 0; }

    // Tiled, prefetch-style streaming: issue UTILE loads up front (unrolled)
    // so many global loads are in flight and DRAM latency is hidden, then
    // insert each into the per-thread register buffer (O(K) cascade).
    constexpr int UTILE = 8;
    const int len = seg_end - seg_start;
    const int stride = BLOCK * UTILE;
    int full = (len / stride) * stride;
    for (int base = 0; base < full; base += stride) {
        float vb[UTILE]; int ib[UTILE];
        #pragma unroll
        for (int j = 0; j < UTILE; j++) {
            int q = base + threadIdx.x + j * BLOCK;
            vb[j] = xrow[seg_start + q];
            ib[j] = seg_start + q;
        }
        #pragma unroll
        for (int j = 0; j < UTILE; j++) insert_topk<K>(tv, ti, vb[j], ib[j]);
    }
    for (int q = full + threadIdx.x; q < len; q += BLOCK) {
        insert_topk<K>(tv, ti, xrow[seg_start + q], seg_start + q);
    }

    // dump sorted-descending per-thread buffers into shmem (stride K+1).
    #pragma unroll
    for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) {
        int off = threadIdx.x * (K + 1) + i;
        sv[off] = tv[i]; si[off] = ti[i];
    }
    __syncthreads();

    merge_tree<BLOCK, K>(sv, si, BLOCK);

    // block top-K now in sv[0..K-1], sorted desc
    if (nseg == 1) {
        for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
            out_vals[(size_t)row * K + i] = sv[i];
            out_idx [(size_t)row * K + i] = (long long)si[i];
        }
    } else {
        size_t slot = ((size_t)row * nseg + seg) * K;
        for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
            tmp_vals[slot + i] = sv[i];
            tmp_idx [slot + i] = (long long)si[i];
        }
    }
}

template <int K, int BLOCK>
__global__ void topk_merge_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ tmp_vals, const long long* __restrict__ tmp_idx,
    float* __restrict__ out_vals, long long* __restrict__ out_idx, int nseg)
{
    const int row = blockIdx.x;
    const size_t base = (size_t)row * nseg * K;

    constexpr int S = K + 1;
    constexpr int M = BLOCK * S;
    extern __shared__ float smem[];
    float* sv = smem;
    int* si = reinterpret_cast<int*>(sv + M);

    // load nseg buffers (each K, sorted desc from reduce stage) into shmem at
    // stride S; pad extra buffers with -INFINITY.
    int nbuf = nseg;
    int np = 1; while (np < nbuf) np <<= 1;
    nbuf = np;   // round up to power of two (merge_tree requirement)
    for (int b = threadIdx.x; b < nbuf; b += BLOCK) {
        int dst = b * S;
        if (b < nseg) {
            int src = b * K;
            #pragma unroll
            for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) {
                sv[dst + i] = tmp_vals[base + src + i];
                si[dst + i] = (int)tmp_idx[base + src + i];
            }
        } else {
            #pragma unroll
            for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { sv[dst + i] = -INFINITY; si[dst + i] = 0; }
        }
    }
    __syncthreads();

    merge_tree<BLOCK, K>(sv, si, nbuf);

    for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
        out_vals[(size_t)row * K + i] = sv[i];
        out_idx [(size_t)row * K + i] = (long long)si[i];
    }
}

// -----------------------------------------------------------------------
// Radix-256 histogram select top-k (one block per row). 4 byte-passes (MSB
// first) build the monotonic uint of the k-th largest via a 256-bin histogram
// per pass; ~12 __syncthreads total vs ~96 for a 1-bit radix, and 4x fewer
// row reads. Far cheaper than the O(n*K) per-thread cascade for large K.
// -----------------------------------------------------------------------

// float -> monotonic uint (larger float -> larger uint). NaN -> 0 (excluded).
__device__ __forceinline__ unsigned f2u(float f) {
    unsigned u = __float_as_int(f);
    if ((u & 0x7f800000u) == 0x7f800000u && (u & 0x007fffffu)) return 0;
    return (u & 0x80000000u) ? ~u : (u | 0x80000000u);
}

// Selects top-k from xv[0..len-1]. For a selected element at position p, its
// reported index is (idx_in ? idx_in[p] : base + p). Writes k results sorted
// descending to shmem gsv[0..k-1], gsi[0..k-1]. Uses external shmem hist[256].
template <int BLOCK, int K>
__device__ __forceinline__ void radix_select(
    const float* xv, const int* idx_in, int base, int len, int k,
    float* gsv, int* gsi, int* hist)
{
    const int tid = threadIdx.x;
    __shared__ unsigned s_thr;
    __shared__ int s_krem;
    if (tid == 0) { s_thr = 0; s_krem = k; }
    __syncthreads();

    unsigned thr = 0;
    int krem = k;
    for (int sh = 24; sh >= 0; sh -= 8) {
        for (int i = tid; i < 256; i += BLOCK) hist[i] = 0;
        __syncthreads();
        unsigned pmask = (sh == 24) ? 0u : (~0u << (sh + 8));
        unsigned pval = thr & pmask;
        for (int p = tid; p < len; p += BLOCK) {
            unsigned u = f2u(xv[p]);
            if ((u & pmask) == pval) atomicAdd(&hist[(u >> sh) & 0xff], 1);
        }
        __syncthreads();
        if (tid == 0) {
            int c = 0, chosen = 0;
            #pragma unroll
            for (int b = 255; b >= 0; b--) {
                if (c + hist[b] >= krem) { chosen = b; break; }
                c += hist[b];
            }
            thr |= (unsigned)(chosen << sh);
            krem = krem - c;
            s_thr = thr; s_krem = (krem < 1) ? 1 : krem;
        }
        __syncthreads();
        thr = s_thr; krem = s_krem;
    }

    __shared__ int wpos;
    if (tid == 0) wpos = 0;
    __syncthreads();
    for (int p = tid; p < len; p += BLOCK) {
        unsigned u = f2u(xv[p]);
        if (u > thr) {
            int pos = atomicAdd(&wpos, 1);
            if (pos < k) { gsv[pos] = xv[p]; gsi[pos] = idx_in ? idx_in[p] : (base + p); }
        }
    }
    __syncthreads();
    if (wpos < k) {
        for (int p = tid; p < len; p += BLOCK) {
            unsigned u = f2u(xv[p]);
            if (u == thr) {
                int pos = atomicAdd(&wpos, 1);
                if (pos < k) { gsv[pos] = xv[p]; gsi[pos] = idx_in ? idx_in[p] : (base + p); }
            }
        }
        __syncthreads();
    }

    // cooperative in-shmem bitonic sort, descending
    for (int size = 2; size <= K; size <<= 1)
        for (int stride = size >> 1; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) {
            for (int i = tid; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
                int j = i ^ stride;
                if (j > i && j < K) {
                    bool up = ((i & size) == 0);
                    if ((up && gsv[i] < gsv[j]) || (!up && gsv[i] > gsv[j])) {
                        float f = gsv[i]; gsv[i] = gsv[j]; gsv[j] = f;
                        int g = gsi[i]; gsi[i] = gsi[j]; gsi[j] = g;
                    }
                }
            }
            __syncthreads();
        }
}

template <int BLOCK, int K>
__global__ void topk_radix_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out_vals,
    long long* __restrict__ out_idx, int n, int k)
{
    const int row = blockIdx.x;
    const float* xr = x + (size_t)row * n;
    __shared__ int hist[256];
    __shared__ float gsv[K];
    __shared__ int gsi[K];
    radix_select<BLOCK, K>(xr, (const int*)0, 0, n, k, gsv, gsi, hist);
    const int tid = threadIdx.x;
    for (int i = tid; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
        out_vals[(size_t)row * K + i] = gsv[i];
        out_idx [(size_t)row * K + i] = (long long)gsi[i];
    }
}

// Segmented radix reduce: grid(batch, nseg). Each block radix-selects top-K
// from its contiguous segment, writes sorted (val, global_idx) to temp.
template <int BLOCK, int K>
__global__ void topk_radix_seg_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ tmp_vals,
    int* __restrict__ tmp_idx, int n, int nseg, int seg_size, int k)
{
    const int row = blockIdx.x;
    const int seg = blockIdx.y;
    const int seg_start = seg * seg_size;
    const int seg_end = (seg_start + seg_size < n) ? (seg_start + seg_size) : n;
    const float* xr = x + (size_t)row * n + seg_start;
    __shared__ int hist[256];
    __shared__ float gsv[K];
    __shared__ int gsi[K];
    radix_select<BLOCK, K>(xr, (const int*)0, seg_start, seg_end - seg_start, k, gsv, gsi, hist);
    const int tid = threadIdx.x;
    size_t slot = ((size_t)row * nseg + seg) * K;
    for (int i = tid; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
        tmp_vals[slot + i] = gsv[i];
        tmp_idx [slot + i] = gsi[i];
    }
}

// Radix merge: grid(batch). Reads nseg*K (val,idx) candidates, radix-selects
// top-K, writes sorted output.
template <int BLOCK, int K>
__global__ void topk_radix_merge_kernel(
    const float* __restrict__ tmp_vals, const int* __restrict__ tmp_idx,
    float* __restrict__ out_vals, long long* __restrict__ out_idx,
    int nseg, int k)
{
    const int row = blockIdx.x;
    const float* tv = tmp_vals + (size_t)row * nseg * K;
    const int* ti = tmp_idx + (size_t)row * nseg * K;
    __shared__ int hist[256];
    __shared__ float gsv[K];
    __shared__ int gsi[K];
    radix_select<BLOCK, K>(tv, ti, 0, nseg * K, k, gsv, gsi, hist);
    const int tid = threadIdx.x;
    for (int i = tid; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
        out_vals[(size_t)row * K + i] = gsv[i];
        out_idx [(size_t)row * K + i] = (long long)gsi[i];
    }
}

#define LAUNCH_RADIX(KB) \
extern "C" void launch_radix_##KB(const float* x, float* ov, long long* oi, \
    int batch, int n, int k) { \
    topk_radix_kernel<1024, KB><<<batch, 1024>>>(x, ov, oi, n, k); \
}
#define LAUNCH_RADIX_BIG(KB) \
extern "C" void launch_radix_big_##KB(const float* x, float* ov, long long* oi, \
    float* tv_, int* ti_, int batch, int n, int nseg, int seg_size, int k) { \
    dim3 g(batch, nseg); \
    topk_radix_seg_kernel<1024, KB><<<g, 1024>>>(x, tv_, ti_, n, nseg, seg_size, k); \
    topk_radix_merge_kernel<1024, KB><<<batch, 1024>>>(tv_, ti_, ov, oi, nseg, k); \
}
LAUNCH_RADIX(8)
LAUNCH_RADIX(16)
LAUNCH_RADIX(32)
LAUNCH_RADIX(64)
LAUNCH_RADIX_BIG(64)

#define LAUNCH_REDUCE(KB, BL) \
extern "C" void launch_reduce_##KB(const float* x, float* ov, long long* oi, \
    float* tv_, long long* ti_, int batch, int n, int nseg, int seg_size) { \
    constexpr int M = BL * (KB + 1); \
    size_t smem = (size_t)M * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)); \
    cudaFuncSetAttribute(topk_reduce_kernel<KB, BL>, \
        cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, 99 * 1024); \
    dim3 grid(batch, nseg); \
    topk_reduce_kernel<KB, BL><<<grid, BL, smem>>>( \
        x, ov, oi, tv_, ti_, batch, n, nseg, seg_size); \
}

#define LAUNCH_MERGE(KB, BL) \
extern "C" void launch_merge_##KB(const float* tv_, const long long* ti_, \
    float* ov, long long* oi, int batch, int nseg) { \
    constexpr int M = BL * (KB + 1); \
    size_t smem = (size_t)M * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)); \
    cudaFuncSetAttribute(topk_merge_kernel<KB, BL>, \
        cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, 99 * 1024); \
    topk_merge_kernel<KB, BL><<<batch, BL, smem>>>(tv_, ti_, ov, oi, nseg); \
}

LAUNCH_REDUCE(1, 256)
LAUNCH_REDUCE(8, 256)
LAUNCH_REDUCE(16, 256)
LAUNCH_REDUCE(32, 256)
LAUNCH_REDUCE(64, 128)
LAUNCH_MERGE(1, 256)
LAUNCH_MERGE(8, 256)
LAUNCH_MERGE(16, 256)
LAUNCH_MERGE(32, 256)
LAUNCH_MERGE(64, 128)

// Fused big-row path: enqueue reduce + merge back-to-back in ONE host call so
// there is no Python round-trip (and GPU idle gap) between the two launches.
#define FUSED_BIG(KB, RBL, MBL) \
extern "C" void launch_big_##KB(const float* x, float* ov, long long* oi, \
    float* tv_, long long* ti_, int batch, int n, int nseg, int seg_size) { \
    constexpr int RM = RBL * (KB + 1); \
    size_t rsmem = (size_t)RM * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)); \
    cudaFuncSetAttribute(topk_reduce_kernel<KB, RBL>, \
        cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, 99 * 1024); \
    dim3 grid(batch, nseg); \
    topk_reduce_kernel<KB, RBL><<<grid, RBL, rsmem>>>( \
        x, ov, oi, tv_, ti_, batch, n, nseg, seg_size); \
    constexpr int MM = MBL * (KB + 1); \
    size_t msmem = (size_t)MM * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)); \
    cudaFuncSetAttribute(topk_merge_kernel<KB, MBL>, \
        cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, 99 * 1024); \
    topk_merge_kernel<KB, MBL><<<batch, MBL, msmem>>>(tv_, ti_, ov, oi, nseg); \
}
FUSED_BIG(64, 128, 128)
"""

_SO = None


def _build_and_load():
    here = os.path.dirname(os.path.abspath(__file__))
    cache_dir = os.path.join(here, "_kb_topk_cache")
    os.makedirs(cache_dir, exist_ok=True)
    so_path = os.path.join(cache_dir, "kb_topk_sm86.so")

    if not os.path.exists(so_path):
        cu_path = os.path.join(cache_dir, "topk.cu")
        with open(cu_path, "w") as f:
            f.write(_CUDA_SRC)
        last = None
        for ccbin in ("/usr/bin/g++-14", "/usr/bin/g++-13", "/usr/bin/g++-12", "g++"):
            cmd = [
                "nvcc", "-O3", "--use_fast_math", "-std=c++17",
                "-arch=sm_86", "-Xcompiler=-fPIC",
                "-ccbin", ccbin, "--shared", cu_path, "-o", so_path,
            ]
            try:
                subprocess.run(cmd, check=True)
                break
            except subprocess.CalledProcessError as e:
                last = e
        else:
            raise RuntimeError(f"nvcc build failed: {last}")

    lib = ctypes.CDLL(so_path)
    for fn in ("launch_reduce_1", "launch_reduce_8", "launch_reduce_16",
               "launch_reduce_32", "launch_reduce_64",
               "launch_merge_1", "launch_merge_8", "launch_merge_16",
               "launch_merge_32", "launch_merge_64",
               "launch_big_64",
               "launch_radix_8", "launch_radix_16", "launch_radix_32",
               "launch_radix_64", "launch_radix_big_64"):
        getattr(lib, fn).restype = None
    return lib


def _lib():
    global _SO
    if _SO is None:
        _SO = _build_and_load()
    return _SO


class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, batch: int, n: int, k: int):
        super().__init__()
        self.batch, self.n, self.k = batch, n, k
        self.register_buffer("_dummy", torch.zeros(1))

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor):
        b, n, k = self.batch, self.n, self.k
        x = x.contiguous()
        out_vals = torch.empty((b, k), dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device)
        out_idx = torch.empty((b, k), dtype=torch.int64, device=x.device)

        lib = _lib()
        xp = ctypes.c_void_p(x.data_ptr())
        ovp = ctypes.c_void_p(out_vals.data_ptr())
        oip = ctypes.c_void_p(out_idx.data_ptr())

        # K<=16: the per-thread cascade is cheaper than radix's fixed pass
        # overhead. K>=32: radix-256 histogram select wins. The single very-large
        # row uses a segmented radix (many blocks reduce, one merges) so the row
        # is reduced with full GPU parallelism instead of one block.
        if k in (1, 8, 16):
            getattr(lib, f"launch_reduce_{k}")(xp, ovp, oip, xp, oip, b, n, 1, n)
        elif n <= _SEG_THRESHOLD:
            getattr(lib, f"launch_radix_{k}")(xp, ovp, oip, b, n, k)
        else:
            seg_size = 2048
            nseg = (n + seg_size - 1) // seg_size
            tmp_vals = torch.empty((b, nseg, k), dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device)
            tmp_idx = torch.empty((b, nseg, k), dtype=torch.int32, device=x.device)
            tvp = ctypes.c_void_p(tmp_vals.data_ptr())
            tip = ctypes.c_void_p(tmp_idx.data_ptr())
            getattr(lib, f"launch_radix_big_{k}")(
                xp, ovp, oip, tvp, tip, b, n, nseg, seg_size, k)

        return out_vals, out_idx
Done. Here's a summary of the work. ## Result `solution.py` passes `check.py` (all 5 shapes × 3 seeds × numeric-stress cases + edge cases) and scores **geomean peak_fraction ≈ 0.0495** — about **2.15× torch.topk** (which itself scores ~0.023). It beats torch.topk on every shape: | shape (b,n,k) | mine | torch.topk | |---|---|---| | (1, 131072, 64) | 54µs | 68µs | | (64, 8192, 8) | 19µs | 71µs | | (32, 16384, 32) | 40µs | 71µs | | (16, 12000, 16) | 30µs | 66µs | | (128, 4096, 1) | 14µs | 41µs | Forbidden-op scan clean, ruff clean, framework = `cuda_raw`. ## Design (shape-adaptive) The problem is launch-overhead-bound (rows are 16KB–512KB; the dispatch floor is ~11µs), so the win is minimizing per-call kernel work, not saturating DRAM. Three kernels, picked per (k, n): - **k ≤ 16** — per-row **register cascade**: one block/row, each thread keeps a size-K sorted-descending register buffer, merged across the block by a log₂(BLOCK) merge-tree in shared memory (stride K+1 to dodge 32-way bank conflicts). - **k ≥ 32** — per-row **radix-256 histogram select**: 4 byte-passes (~12 barriers, not ~96 like 1-bit radix which is sync-bound), then in-shmem bitonic sort. - **n = 131072** — **segmented radix**: G blocks each radix-select their segment, one block merges. ## Hard-won gotchas (the real work) 1. **Register spill kills it**: dynamic indexing `tv[q]` into a register array spills to local memory (1389 `.local` refs → 10× slower). Fixed with a fully-unrolled conditional-cascade using static indices only. A shmem min-heap (O(log K)) was *slower* than the O(K) register cascade due to dependent shmem-load latency. 2. **gcc-15/16 can't build torch extensions** — torch 2.11's `List_inl.h` is ill-formed (missing `typename`). Worked around by compiling a standalone `.so` with `extern "C"` + `nvcc -ccbin g++-14`, called via ctypes with raw `data_ptr()` — never includes `torch/extension.h`. 3. **Bank conflicts** in the merge-tree (stride-K buffers, K=32 → 32-way conflict) fixed with K+1 stride. 4. **Load-latency hiding** via tiled prefetch in the streaming loop. The biggest remaining drag is shape 0 (single 131072-row, k=64) at ~0.012 — fundamentally hard without a cooperative-grid radix (fiddly via ctypes, not attempted). That caps geomean around ~0.07–0.09 even with all other shapes at the dispatch floor.

"""Canonical shape sweep for TopK.

Mix of:
  - decoder vocab top-k (single sequence, very large n, moderate k) — pure
    bandwidth test; the input read dominates everything.
  - prefill / batched attention top-k (many rows, moderate n, small k) — tests
    per-row parallelism and shared-memory bitonic networks.

    powers of two; this forces the agent to handle padding or partial sorts.

import torch, time
torch.cuda.set_device(0)
SHAPES = [
    (1, 131072, 64),
    (64, 8192, 8),
    (32, 16384, 32),
    (16, 12000, 16),
    (128, 4096, 1),
]
PEAK = 936.0  # GB/s DRAM 3090
def bench(fn, iters=100):
    # warmup
    for _ in range(20): fn()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    s=time.time()
    for _ in range(iters): fn()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    return (time.time()-s)/iters*1000

for (b,n,k) in SHAPES:
    x = torch.randn(b,n,device='cuda',dtype=torch.float32)
    ms = bench(lambda: torch.topk(x,k,sorted=True))
    bytes_moved = b*n*4 + b*k*(4+8)
    gbps = bytes_moved/ms/1e6
    print(f"b={b:4d} n={n:6d} k={k:3d}  topk ms={ms:.4f}  gbps={gbps:.1f}  frac={gbps/PEAK:.3f}")

"""Centralized GPU timing for benchmark.py files.

All problems' benchmark.py should call `time_fn` from here so we have one
implementation of warmup, L2 flush, and CUDA event capture to maintain.

Methodology:
  - 10 warmup calls absorb Triton autotune (typical ~7 configs) and
    torch.compile reduce-overhead CUDA-graph capture.
  - Between each timed call, `_l2_flush()` writes 128 MB to evict L2
    (Blackwell L2 is 96 MB; we want any prior L2 contents flushed so
    we measure HBM-load bandwidth, not L2-cached bandwidth).
  - GPU timing via cuda.Event with synchronize() AFTER record() but
    BEFORE elapsed_time() — the canonical NVIDIA pattern.
  - Reported value is the median of `iters` trials, robust to outliers.

Notes / known biases not addressed here:
  - torch.compile(mode="reduce-overhead") gets CUDA graphs which eliminate
    launch overhead. Custom Triton/CUDA kernels do NOT get this treatment.
    On small shapes where launch overhead matters, this gives compile an
    artificial advantage. We accept this as the cost of using the agreed
    "compiled" baseline policy.
  - cuBLAS / cuDNN allocate workspaces on first call. 10 warmup absorbs.
  - Median over a fairly small number of trials (default 30) is fine for
    headline numbers but won't catch bimodal distributions. Use --extra
    instrumentation if you ever care.
"""
from __future__ import annotations

import os
import re
import statistics
import time
from datetime import UTC, dat

"""Numeric distribution stress cases for correctness checks.

These cases are not hidden shapes. They rerun the same shape/seed validation
under a few scale regimes that catch kernels that only work for the nominal
N(0, 1)-ish inputs.
"""
from __future__ import annotations

import os
from collections.abc import Iterator, Mapping, Sequence
from contextlib import contextmanager
from dataclasses import dataclass, field

import torch

ToleranceOverride = Mapping[str, float | Mapping[str, float]]


@dataclass(frozen=True)
class NumericStressCase:
    name: str
    input_scales: Mapping[int, float] = field(default_factory=dict)
    state_scales: Mapping[str, float] = field(default_factory=dict)
    tolerance: ToleranceOverride | None = None

_LARGE_FP32 = {"float32": {"atol": 1e-1, "rtol": 1e-4}}

+"""Single-pass top-k for RTX 3090 (SM86).
+
+Strategy: torch.topk uses multi-pass radix select (one pass per radix digit,
+~8 passes for fp32). For these shapes the input is small enough that launch /
+pass overhead dominates, so a SINGLE-pass kernel that reads the row once and
+selects top-k in one launch beats it comfortably.
+
+Design:
+  - block-per-row (one block reduces a whole row to top-k).
+  - each thread streams its strided slice of the row, keeping a per-thread
+    sorted-ascending register buffer of size K (the K largest it has seen,
+    tv[0] = smallest = threshold).
+  - block-merge: dump BLOCK*K candidates into shared mem, bitonic-sort
+    descending, take the first K.
+  - for the single very-large row (n=131072, batch=1) one block cannot
+    saturate DRAM, so a segmented two-launch path is used: G blocks each
+    reduce a contiguous segment to top-K into a temp buffer, then one block
+    bitonic-merges G*K candidates.
+
+Correctness: per-thread buffer size == K guarantees the global top-K survives
+to the candidate pool (any single thread/segment sees at most K of the global
+top-K elements, since only K elements are >= the global k-th value), and the
+bitonic sort extracts exactly the top-K sorted descending.
+"""
+from __future__ import annotations
+
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline
+
+OP_TYPE = "topk"
+SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["fp32"]
+HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_3090"]
+
+# Threshold: rows with n above this are reduced via the multi-block segmented
+# path (one block can't saturate DRAM bandwidth on a huge row).
+_SEG_THRESHOLD = 1 << 15  # 32768
+
+_BLOCK = 256          # threads for the per-row kernel
+_REDUCE_BLOCK = 128   # threads for the segmented reduce kernel
+
+_CUDA_SRC = r"""
+#include <torch/extension.h>
+#include <cuda.h>
+#include <cuda_runtime.h>
+
+// Per-thread: insert v (global index gi) into a sorted-ascending register
+// buffer tv[0..K-1] (tv[0]=smallest=threshold). Drops the old smallest.
+template <int K>
+__device__ __forceinline__ void insert_topk(float (&tv)[K], int (&ti)[K], float v, int gi) {
+    if (v <= tv[0]) return;
+    int q = 1;
+    while (q < K && tv[q] < v) { tv[q - 1] = tv[q]; ti[q - 1] = ti[q]; q++; }
+    tv[q - 1] = v; ti[q - 1] = gi;
+}
+
+// Bitonic sort of M (power of two) (val,int) pairs in shared mem, DESCENDING by val.
+// sv/si are the value / index arrays of length M. Padded slots hold -INFINITY.
+template <int BLOCK>
+__device__ __forceinline__ void bitonic_sort_desc(float* sv, int* si, int M) {
+    for (int size = 2; size <= M; size <<= 1) {
+        for (int stride = size >> 1; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) {
+            for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < M; i += BLOCK) {
+                int j = i ^ stride;
+                if (j > i) {
+                    bool up = ((i & size) == 0);     // first half of size-block: descending
+                    float ai = sv[i], aj = sv[j];
+                    bool swap = up ? (ai < aj) : (ai > aj);
+                    if (swap) {
+                        sv[i] = aj; sv[j] = ai;
+                        int t = si[i]; si[i] = si[j]; si[j] = t;
+                    }
+                }
+            }
+            __syncthreads();
+        }
+    }
+}
+
+// -----------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Per-row / per-segment top-k reduction kernel.
+//   grid = (batch, nseg).  nseg==1 -> write final output. nseg>1 -> temp.
+// -----------------------------------------------------------------------
+template <int K, int BLOCK>
+__global__ void topk_reduce_kernel(
+    const float* __restrict__ x,       // [batch, n]
+    float* __restrict__ out_vals,      // [batch, K]   (nseg==1)
+    int64_t* __restrict__ out_idx,     // [batch, K]   (nseg==1)
+    float* __restrict__ tmp_vals,      // [batch, nseg, K] (nseg>1)
+    int64_t* __restrict__ tmp_idx,     // [batch, nseg, K] (nseg>1)
+    int batch, int n, int nseg, int seg_size)
+{
+    const int row = blockIdx.x;
+    const int seg = blockIdx.y;
+    if (row >= batch) return;
+    const int seg_start = seg * seg_size;
+    const int seg_end = (seg_start + seg_size < n) ? (seg_start + seg_size) : n;
+    const float* xrow = x + (int64_t)row * n;
+
+    float tv[K]; int ti[K];
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { tv[i] = -INFINITY; ti[i] = 0; }
+
+    for (int p = threadIdx.x; p < (seg_end - seg_start); p += BLOCK) {
+        insert_topk<K>(tv, ti, xrow[seg_start + p], seg_start + p);
+    }
+
+    constexpr int M = BLOCK * K;
+    extern __shared__ float smem[];
+    float* sv = smem;
+    int* si = reinterpret_cast<int*>(sv + M);
+
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) {
+        int off = threadIdx.x * K + i;
+        sv[off] = tv[i]; si[off] = ti[i];
+    }
+    __syncthreads();
+
+    bitonic_sort_desc<BLOCK>(sv, si, M);
+
+    if (nseg == 1) {
+        for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
+            out_vals[(int64_t)row * K + i] = sv[i];
+            out_idx [(int64_t)row * K + i] = (int64_t)si[i];
+        }
+    } else {
+        int64_t slot = ((int64_t)row * nseg + seg) * K;
+        for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < K; i += BLOCK) {
+            tmp_vals[slot + i] = sv[i];
+            tmp_idx [slot + i] = (int64_t)si[i];
+        }
+    }
+}
+
+// -----------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Merge kernel: read [batch, nseg, K] candidates, bitonic-sort, write top-K.
+// -----------------------------------------------------------------------
+template <int K, int BLOCK>
+__global__ void topk_merge_kernel(
+    const float* __restrict__ tmp_vals,   // [batch, nseg, K]
+    const int64_t* __restrict__ tmp_idx,
+    float* __restrict__ out_vals,         // [batch, K]
+    int64_t* 

... (6611 more chars)

#!/bin/bash
# torch 2.11.0 + inductor CSE typing hotfix.
#
# torch._inductor.codegen.cutedsl.cutedsl_kernel.CuteDSLSubgraphInfo has:
#     cse: Optional[CSE[Any]] = None
# but CSE is Generic[CSEVariableType, AugmentedKeyT] (two params). On any
# torch.compile call that touches the cutedsl codegen path (which fires on
# Blackwell SM120), this raises TypeError at class definition time.
#
# Upstream fix landed in main branch; not yet in 2.11.0 release. Patch locally.
# Re-run this after every `uv sync` or torch upgrade.
#
# See https://github.com/pytorch/pytorch/issues/174094

set -euo pipefail

patch_file() {
    local f="$1"
    if [ ! -f "$f" ]; then
        return 0
    fi
    if grep -q "cse: Optional\[CSE\[Any, Any\]\] = None" "$f"; then
        echo "  [skip] already patched: $f"
        return 0
    fi
    if grep -q "cse: Optional\[CSE\[Any\]\] = None" "$f"; then
        sed -i.bak 's|cse: Optional\[CSE\[Any\]\] = None|cse: Optional[CSE[Any, Any]] = None|' "$f"
        # Remove stale pyc
        rm -f "$(dirname "$f")/__pycache__/cutedsl_kernel.cpython-"*.pyc
        echo "  [fix]  patched: $f"
    else
        echo "  [skip] line not found (already a newer torch?): $f"
    fi
}

echo "Applying torch inductor CSE typing hotfix..."

# Venv torch (uv creates .venv by default)
if [ -d .venv ]; then
    for f in .venv/lib/python*/site-packages/torch/_inductor/codegen/cutedsl/cutedsl_kernel.py; do
        patch_file "$f"
    done
fi

# System-wide torch (user install, python

Instead, load the whole row into shared memory and do block-level top-K selection via a thresholding approach.** Threshold approach (radix-free, single-block): 1. Load row into shmem (coalesced). 2. Find approximate threshold T (e.g., via sampling or just max). 3. Count elements ≥ T, adjust T, iterate. This is essentially radix select but value-based. Converges in a few iterations. Each iteration reads shmem once. Fast. But implementing robust threshold selection is fiddly. Simplest robust + fast: **block-level bitonic sort of the row in shmem, take top-K.** But we saw bitonic full-sort has many barriers. However, for the row sizes here (≤16384), and shmem-resident, let me reconsider its actual cost. The earlier bitonic was over M=BLOCK*K candidate pool (8192) giving 0.6ms. That was suspiciously slow — maybe also due to spill? No, bitonic used shmem sv/si with runtime i (shmem, fine). The 0.6ms was likely bank conflicts (strided j=i^stride access) + barrier count. Hmm. Let me reconsider. Let me just fix the spill first (move per-thread buffer to shmem) and re-measure — that alone might fix most of it since 1389 local refs = tons of DRAM traffic per thread. Actually wait — a cleaner idea to avoid BOTH spill and per-element O(K) shifts: **use a register-resident buffer but access it without dynamic indexing by keeping it sorted via a compile-time sorting network applied after batching.** Specifically: each thread maintains tv[K] in registers. For a batch of K new elements, merge the sorted tv with the batch. But the batch also needs sorting. And merging two sorted arrays of size K can be done with a compile-time network (od-even merge) without dynamic indexing. This is the "merge path" approach. This is getting complex. Let me go with the shmem per-thread buffer — simplest, no spill, and shmem is fast. Let me restructure: Shared memory layout: - `sv[BLOCK*K]`: per-thread value buffers (sv[tid*K + i]) - `si[BLOCK*K]`: per-thread index buffers Insertion: thread tid inserts into sv[tid*K..], using dynamic shmem indexing. Keep sorted descending, sv[tid*K+K-1] = threshold. Then merge_tree as before (operates on the same shmem, treating each thread's buffer as one of BLOCK buffers). Wait, but this means EVERY element insertion hits shmem. For n=16384, BLOCK=256: 64 elements/thread * O(K) shmem ops. With K=32: 2048 shmem read+write ops/thread, each ~4 bytes. 256 threads * 2048 * 8 bytes = 4MB shmem traffic per block. Shmem bandwidth on sm_86 ~ tens of TB/s. 4MB / 10TB/s = 0.4us. Fast enough. Plus the comparison latency. Should be fine. But actually, do I even need per-thread buffers + merge? With the row in shmem already... no, the row is too big for shmem for shape 0. For per-row shapes (n≤16384) the row fits. Let me consider TWO strategies: For per-row shapes (n ≤ 16384, fits in ~64KB shmem): load row to shmem, do a block-wide top-K selection. For shape 0 (n=131072): segmented with per-segment shmem. Actually, let me unify on the per-thread-buffer-in-shmem approach since it works for all n (each thread streams from global, no need to fit row in shmem). Let me implement that. Let me rewrite. Per-thread buffer in shmem, insertion with dynamic indexing (shmem, no spill), then merge_tree. Concern: the insertion shift in shmem — `while(q>=0 && sv[base+q] < v) { sv[base+q+1]=sv[base+q]; ...}`. This is sequential shifts in shmem. Each shift is a shmem read+write. For K=32, worst case 31 shifts per insert. 64 inserts * 31 = ~2000 shmem R+W /thread. OK. Actually, let me reduce insertion cost with a smarter structure: keep the per-thread buffer as a min-heap with the min at a KNOWN position, but heap sift-down uses dynamic indexing too (shmem, fine, but O(log K) instead of O(K)). Heap in shmem: insert = replace root + sift down. O(log K) shmem ops. For K=32: 5 levels. 64 inserts * 5 = 320 shmem ops/thread. Better than 2000. Let me use a min-heap in shmem per thread (heap[0] = smallest = threshold). Insert: if v > heap[0], heap[0]=v, sift_down. At the end, the heap holds top-K but unsorted; I need them sorted for merge_tree (which requires sorted buffers). So after streaming, sort each thread's heap (in shmem) descending via a compile-time sort network? That needs the heap sorted. Hmm, merge_tree needs sorted buffers. Alternatively, make merge_tree work on heaps... no, merging two heaps keeping top-K isn't a simple merge. Let me reconsider: merge_tree needs each buffer sorted. So after the heap phase, sort each thread's K elements. Sorting K elements per thread: a compile-time bitonic sort network (no dynamic index, fully unrolled) — for K=32 that's 32*5*... comparators, no spill, all in registers OR shmem. If in shmem with compile-time indices, fine. This is getting complicated. Let me just go with: per-thread buffer in shmem, sorted-descending maintained via insertion (O(K) shmem per insert, dynamic index OK in shmem). Simpler. Measure. If still slow, optimize insertion. Actually, simplest of all: keep per-thread buffer in registers BUT avoid dynamic indexing by NOT maintaining it sorted during streaming. Instead: thread streams all its elements into... can't, too many. OK let me just do shmem per-thread buffer with insertion-sort. Let me also reconsider: maybe I can keep registers but fix the dynamic index. The compiler spills because of `tv[q]` with runtime q. If I write the insertion with a fixed sequence of conditional moves (no runtime array index), no spill. Insertion into sorted-descending tv[K] (tv[K-1]=threshold), inserting v>threshold: ``` // shift down all elements smaller than v by one, then place v. // Process from the bottom (threshold) upward. float carry = v; int carry_i = gi; for (int i = K-1; i >= 0; i--) { // fully unrolled, static i if (carry > tv[i]) { // tv[i] < v, so tv[i] shifts down, carry continues float t = tv[i]; tv[i] = carry; carry = t; int ti_t = ti[i]; ti[i] = carry_i; carry_i = ti_t; } } ``` Wait, this is a conditional swap cascade. Let me think: tv sorted descending. Insert v (v > tv[K-1]). We want to shift the suffix {tv[i] : tv[i] < v} down by one and place v at the top of that suffix. Cascade from bottom: carry=v. i=K-1: if carry>tv[K-1] (true since v>threshold), swap(carry, tv[K-1]) → now carry=old tv[K-1], tv[K-1]=v. i=K-2: if carry(=old threshold) > tv[K-2]? tv[K-2] ≥ old threshold (sorted desc), so carry ≤ tv[K-2], no swap, and from here no more swaps. Hmm that only placed v at position K-1, not correct. Let me redo. I want: find the largest i such that tv[i] < v... no. tv sorted desc: tv[0]≥tv[1]≥...≥tv[K-1]. Insert v. v belongs at position p where tv[p-1]≥v>tv[p]. Elements tv[p..K-2] shift to tv[p+1..K-1], tv[K-1] dropped, tv[p]=v. Cascade from top: carry=v. i=0: if tv[0] < v? if v is the new max, tv[0]<v, swap → tv[0]=v, carry=old tv[0]. else (tv[0]≥v) no swap, carry stays v but we still need to check lower... This cascade approach: we want to shift down. Let me cascade from top with "once we start shifting, shift everything below": Actually the clean conditional-cascade for insertion (like insertion sort single step): ``` float carry = v; int carry_i = gi; bool placed = false; for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { // unrolled if (!placed && carry >= tv[i]) { // insert here: tv[i] moves down, carry takes its place float t = tv[i]; tv[i] = carry; carry = t; int ti_t = ti[i]; ti[i] = carry_i; carry_i = ti_t; placed = true; // now just shift the rest down } else if (placed) { // continue shifting float t = tv[i]; tv[i] = carry; carry = t; int ti_t = ti[i]; ti[i] = carry_i; carry_i = ti_t; } } ``` The `placed` branch makes it dynamic (predicated). With unrolled i, the compiler can predicate these. `tv[i]` static index → register, no spill! The `placed` flag creates a dependency chain but that's fine (it's a short K-long chain). Simpler unified version (no placed flag): always cascade — but that would move all elements. Let me use the carry approach where carry propagates only once started: ``` float carry = v; int carry_i = gi; bool active = false; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { if (carry > tv[i]) active = true; // v (or carried) is larger than tv[i] if (active) { float t = tv[i]; tv[i] = carry; carry = t; int t2 = ti[i]; ti[i] = carry_i; carry_i = t2; } } ``` Wait, this has the issue: once active, it swaps carry with tv[i] for ALL subsequent i, shifting everything down by one and dropping... no. Let me trace: tv=[10,8,5,3], K=4, insert v=7 (threshold tv[3]=3, 7>3 ok). carry=7, active=false. i=0: carry(7)>tv[0](10)? no. active stays false. i=1: carry(7)>tv[1](8)? no. false. i=2: carry(7)>tv[2](5)? yes → active=true. swap: tv[2]=7, carry=5. i=3: active. swap: tv[3]=carry=5, carry=3(dropped). Result tv=[10,8,7,5]. Correct! (top-4 of {10,8,5,3,7} = 10,8,7,5). ✓ The `if (carry > tv[i]) active=true` uses carry which changes. Once active it stays active (monotonic). Good. And `active` becomes a compile-time-unrollable predicate chain. tv[i] static index → registers, no spill. This is clean. Let me use this for insert_topk, keeping tv[K] sorted descending in registers with NO dynamic indexing. Now merge_tree: it has `tmp[K]` accessed by `tmp[c]` (c unrolled) and reads `A[ia]` (ia runtime, A is shmem — fine). Writes tmp[c] back to A[c] (c unrolled). Reading tmp later: `A[c]=tmp[c]` static c. So tmp[K] in registers, static index → no spill. But wait, does `A[ia]` with runtime ia into shmem cause issues? No, shmem dynamic indexing is fine (it's just a load from shared). So the ONLY spill source was insert_topk's dynamic `tv[q]`. Fix it with the cascade. Actually, wait — merge_tree is templated <BLOCK,K> and uses `float tmp[K]; int ti2[K];` as local arrays. With K=64, that's 128 regs for tmp/ti2, plus the kernel's own tv[K]/ti[K] (another 128).  // Per-thread: insert v (global index gi) into a sorted-DESCENDING register
 // buffer tv[0..K-1] (tv[0]=largest, tv[K-1]=smallest=threshold). Drops old min.
+// Uses a fully-unrolled conditional-cascade with STATIC indices only -- dynamic
+// indexing into a register array would spill it to local memory (very slow).
 template <int K>
 __device__ __forceinline__ void insert_topk(float* tv, int* ti, float v, int gi) {
     if (v <= tv[K - 1]) return;

+        if (active) {
+            float tf = tv[i]; tv[i] = carry; carry = tf;
+            int t2 = ti[i]; ti[i] = carry_i; carry_i = t2;

Maybe register pressure (98 regs for K=32 reduce) limits occupancy. 98 regs/thread * 256 threads = 25088 regs/block. SM has 65536 → 2 blocks/SM. 32 blocks / 82 SMs... only need 16 SMs. So occupancy isn't the limiter (each SM has 2 blocks, plenty). Wait, maybe the issue is that the per-thread insert cascade is actually still spilling or the `active` predicate creates a long chain. Let me reconsider: actually maybe the bottleneck is that for shape 2, the data read is from DRAM (L2 flushed), 16384*4*32 = 2MB across 32 blocks. 2MB/936GB/s = 2.1us ideal. So read isn't the bottleneck. It's compute (insert + merge). Let me profile shape 2 with ncu to see where time goes. Actually, let me reconsider the insert cascade cost. For each element, the cascade runs K=32 iterations unrolled, each doing a comparison + conditional swap. That's 32 dependent steps (carry propagates). So per element ~32 cycles latency (if fully dependent). 64 elements = 2048 cycles serial = 1.4us. Not 70us. So something else. Let me actually profile. Let me also reconsider: maybe merge_tree is slow because at each level, only half the threads work, and there are serialization issues. Let me check with ncu. Actually, let me reconsider the merge_tree indexing for bank conflicts. Buffer b is at sv[b*K]. For K=32, buffer b occupies sv[32b .. 32b+31]. Thread tid at level d=1 merges buffers 2tid and 2tid+1: reads sv[64tid..64tid+31] and sv[64tid+32..+63]. Within a warp (tid 0-31): thread 0 reads addr 0-31, thread 1 reads 64-95, ... These are 32-float-strided → for warp reading sv[64*tid + something], the 32 threads access addresses 0, 64, 128, ... → all same bank? bank = (addr/4) % 32. addr in floats. sv[0], sv[64], sv[128]... bank = (0,64,128,...)%32 = (0,0,0,...) all bank 0! Massive bank conflict (32-way). THAT's the problem. K=32 (and the buffer stride being a multiple of 32 floats = 128 bytes = exactly the bank pattern period) causes 32-way bank conflicts in merge_tree. K=32 → stride 32 floats → bank conflict. K=16 → stride 16 → conflict too. K=8 → stride 8. So merge_tree has severe bank conflicts because consecutive buffers are accessed by consecutive threads with stride = K floats, and K (or K*something) aligns to 32-bank period. For K=32: stride 32 floats = 128 bytes. Banks are 4 bytes, 32 banks = 128-byte period. So stride of 128 bytes = same bank → 32-way conflict. Fix: pad the buffer stride to avoid the 32-bank alignment. Use stride = K+1 (pad each buffer by 1) so consecutive buffers land in different banks. Or pad to K + PAD where PAD breaks alignment. Let me use stride S = K rounded up to avoid multiples of 32. Simplest: S = K + 1 (one float padding per buffer). Then shmem size = BLOCK*(K+1). And buffer b at sv[b*(K+1)]. But wait, also the per-thread DUMP writes sv[tid*K + i] with i=0..K-1 — within a warp, consecutive tids write to consecutive K-strided positions, but i is the same across threads, so warp writes sv[tid*K] for the same i → stride K, same bank issue. For the dump it's one store per i, 32-way conflict → slow too. Hmm. So both dump and merge have K-stride access by consecutive threads. Better fix: change the data layout so consecutive threads access consecutive addresses. Instead of buffer[tid*K+i], lay out so that thread tid's element i is at a coalesced position. Option: swizzle / transpose. Layout: sv[i*BLOCK + tid] so that for fixed i, consecutive tids are consecutive (coalesced). But then buffer tid is scattered (stride BLOCK). For merge_tree, a thread reads its own buffer[tid] across i — that's stride BLOCK, also conflicts? sv[i*BLOCK+tid]: reading buffer of thread tid means addresses tid, BLOCK+tid, 2BLOCK+tid,... stride BLOCK=256 floats=1024 bytes → bank = (256,512,...)%32... 256%32=0, so all bank 0 again. Conflict. The fundamental issue: per-thread buffers of size K, accessed by one thread sequentially (merge) and by all threads in parallel (dump). Can't have both coalesced with a simple layout. Standard solution: **warp-level work.** Reduce within a warp first (using shfl, no shmem), then only cross-warp via shmem with few buffers. With shfl, the per-thread buffer is in registers, and merging uses shfl_xor which has no bank conflict. This is the CUB approach. But shfl merge of K>1 elements per thread is more complex. Let me think about a simpler shmem layout fix. Alternative: keep the K-stride layout but pad stride to a value coprime-ish to 32. Stride S where S mod 32 != 0 and S not causing conflicts. If S=K+1: for K=32, S=33, bank pattern for consecutive buffers: tid 0..31 access sv[tid*33 + i] = banks ((tid*33)%32) = (tid*1)%32 (since 33≡1 mod 32) → banks 0,1,2,...,31 → NO conflict! Because 33 mod 32 = 1. So padding stride by +1 (S=K+1) makes consecutive-buffer access (by consecutive threads) hit consecutive banks. For the DUMP (all threads same i, stride S=K+1): same fix → coalesced. But for merge, a single thread reads its buffer sequentially: sv[tid*S + 0..K-1] → consecutive addresses → coalesced for that thread (no conflict, it's one thread reading sequentially). Good. And the per-thread insert cascade is in registers (tv), not shmem. The dump writes tv[i] to sv[tid*S+i]. Good. So fix: pad buffer stride to S = K+1. Let me verify for K=8: S=9, 9 mod 32 = 9, consecutive buffers banks differ by 9 → 0,9,18,27,4,13,22,31,6,15,24,1,... all distinct mod 32 for 32 buffers (since gcd(9,32)=1). No conflict. K=16: S=17, gcd(17,32)=1, no conflict. K=1: S=2, banks differ by 2, 32 threads → banks 0,2,4,...,30,0,2,... 2-way conflict (16 distinct). Minor. K=64: S=65, 65 mod 32 = 1, no conflict. Great, S=K+1 fixes bank conflicts for all K. Let me implement: use stride S=K+1 in both reduce and merge kernels. Shmem size = nbuf * S * (4+4 bytes) for values+indices? Actually values and indices in separate arrays. sv: nbuf*S floats, si: nbuf*S ints. Wait, but I also need the per-thread insertion to stay in registers (it does — tv[K] is the register buffer, dumped to shmem after). The dump: sv[tid*S + i] = tv[i]. Then merge_tree operates on buffers of stride S. The merge reads A = sv + base*S, B = sv + (base+d)*S, K consecutive elements each. Sequential read by one thread → fine. Let me update merge_tree to use stride S instead of K. And the dump. And the merge_kernel load. Let me reconsider: in merge_tree, `A[c] = tmp[c]` writes to A = sv+base*S, indices 0..K-1 → sv[base*S + 0..K-1]. The padding slot (index K) is untouched (garbage), never read (merge only reads 0..K-1). Good. Let me also reconsider whether this is really the bottleneck or if there's something else. Let me just implement the padding and measure. It's a clean fix. Actually, let me reconsider shape 2 = 0.070ms vs shape 1 = 0.020ms. Shape 1: K=8, n=8192, BLOCK=256. Shape 2: K=32, n=16384. Both 256 threads. The difference: K=8 vs K=32 (4x cascade depth, 4x merge work) and n 8192 vs 16384 (2x elements). So ~8x more work → 0.020*~3.5=0.070. Plausible that it's just more work, but bank conflicts amplify. Let me also reconsider: is the per-thread insert cascade the bottleneck or merge? Let me just fix banks and also try to reduce per-element work. Actually, let me profile with ncu to be sure before more changes. Let me do a quick ncu on shape 2. command (975 chars)
cd /home/infatoshi/kernelbench.com/benchmarks/hard/outputs/runs/20260618_134532_zai-claude_glm-5.2_05_topk_bitonic/repo/problems/05_topk_bitonic
cat > prof2.py << 'EOF'
import torch, ctypes, solution, statistics
lib = solution._lib()
def ev(): return torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)

def time_op(fn, iters=200):
    for _ in range(30): fn()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    ts=[]
    for _ in range(iters):
        s,e=ev(),ev(); s.record(); fn(); e.record(); torch.cuda.synchronize(); ts.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    return statistics.median(ts)*1000  # us

for (b,n,k) in [(32,16384,32),(64,8192,8),(16,12000,16),(128,4096,1),(1,131072,64)]:
    x = torch.randn(b,n,device='cuda')
    m = solution.Model(b,n,k).cuda().eval()
    full = time_op(lambda: m(x))
    # also torch.topk
    tk = time_op(lambda: torch.topk(x,k,sorted=True))
    print(f"b={b} n={n} k={k}: mine={full:.2f}us  topk={tk:.2f}us")
EOF

cat > prof3.py << 'EOF'
import torch, statistics, solution
def ev(): return torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
def t(fn,it=200):
    for _ in range(30): fn()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    a=[]
    for _ in range(it):
        s,e=ev(),ev(); s.record(); fn(); e.record(); torch.cuda.synchronize(); a.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    return statistics.median(a)*1000
# scale n with K=32 fixed, batch=32
print("K=32 batch=32, vary n:")
for n in [4096,8192,16384,32768]:
    x=torch.randn(32,n,device='cuda'); m=solution.Model(32,n,32).cuda().eval()
    print(f"  n={n}: {t(lambda:m(x)):.1f}us")
# scale K with n=8192 fixed, batch=64
print("n=8192 batch=64, vary K:")
for k in [1,8,16,32,64]:
    x=torch.randn(64,8192,device='cuda'); m=solution.Model(64,8192,k).cuda().eval()
    print(f"  K={k}: {t(lambda:m(x)):.1f}us")
# launch floor: empty-ish kernel (K=1, tiny n)
x=torch.randn(128,64,device='cuda'); m=solution.Model(128,64,1).cuda().eval()
print(f"floor K=1 n=64 batch=128: {t(lambda:m(x)):.1f}us")
EOF

reasoning (6976 chars)
Floor is ~11.5us. Time scales roughly linearly with both n and K. K=32, n doubling 4096→8192→16384→32768: 27.6→36.9→56.5→96.1. The increment per doubling n: ~9us (4k→8k), ~20us (8k→16k), ~40us (16k→32k). Roughly linear in n (each +4096 elements adds ~5us per 4k... actually 96-27.6 = 68.5us for 8x n (4k→32k) = ~8.5us per 4096 elements). K scaling (n=8192): K 1→8→16→32→64: 11.5→16.4→23.6→36.9→62.5. K=1 is floor. K=8 adds 5us. K=16 +7us. K=32 +13us. K=64 +25us. Roughly linear in K. So time ≈ floor + a*n + b*K. Both n and K contribute. The n-dependence (~8.5us per 4096 elements per block-row... wait batch=32). Total elements = 32*n. For n=16384: 524k elements in 56us. The insert cascade: most elements below threshold (1 compare). So insert ~ n comparisons per thread. n=16384/256threads = 64/thread. 64 comparisons. Cheap. So why ~8.5us per 4096 elements? Hmm, 8.5us per 4096 elements * 32 rows... no, per row it's 4096 elements. The READ of 4096*4=16KB per row. 32 rows = 512KB. From DRAM 512KB/936GB/s = 0.55us. But measured increment is 8.5us. So read is 15x slower than DRAM peak — meaning we're NOT saturating DRAM. Why? With 32 blocks (one per row), 2 blocks/SM on 16 SMs. Each block reads 64KB. The issue: only 32 blocks → limited parallelism, and per-block the read is serial-ish (each thread streams). But 32 blocks * 256 threads = 8192 threads, plenty for bandwidth... Wait, maybe the L2 flush (128MB) means the 64KB row IS in DRAM and reading it should be fast. But the per-thread insert loop has the element-read inside a dependent chain? No, the read is `xrow[seg_start+p]` then insert. The read can be pipelined (many outstanding). But the insert cascade for filling elements is dependent. During FILL (first K=32 elements/thread), each does a 32-deep cascade. 32 elements * 32-deep = 1024 dependent ops before the thread can issue more reads. This serializes the read pipeline during fill. But fill is only first 32/64 = half the elements. Hmm. Actually the bigger issue might be: the read is NOT coalesced/vectorized. Each thread reads one float per iteration with stride BLOCK. That's a coalesced 128-byte transaction per warp per iteration. For 64 iterations, 64 transactions. That should be efficient. But maybe latency: each iteration the warp issues a load, waits, then inserts. If insert is dependent on the load (it is: v=load; insert(v)), then each iteration is load-latency-bound (~400 cycles for DRAM). 64 iterations * 400 cycles = 25k cycles = 18us! That matches the n-scaling (~8.5us per 4096/256=16 iterations per thread... 16*400cycles/1.4G = 4.5us, plus fill cascade). So the kernel is LATENCY-bound on global memory loads, because each thread serially: load → insert → load → insert, with the insert depending on the load. The loads don't overlap enough because there's only one outstanding load per thread at a time (load, use, load). FIX: **software pipelining / prefetching.** Issue multiple loads ahead before consuming them. Read a batch of elements into registers (e.g., 8 at a time via a small loop), THEN insert them. This hides latency by having many outstanding loads. Better: **vectorized loads (float4)** + prefetch. Read 4 floats at once, issue several ahead. Even better: each thread reads its chunk into a register buffer in batches, with loads pipelined, then processes. Let me restructure the streaming loop: ``` // process in tiles of TILE elements, prefetching for (int t = tid; t < len; t += BLOCK*TILE) { float buf[TILE]; #pragma unroll for (int j=0;j<TILE;j++){ int idx = t + j*BLOCK; // hmm stride buf[j] = (idx<len) ? xrow[seg_start+idx] : -INFINITY; } #pragma unroll for (int j=0;j<TILE;j++) insert_topk<K>(tv,ti,buf[j], ...); } ``` With TILE=8 and unrolled, the 8 loads issue before any insert → 8 outstanding loads → hides ~8x latency. The compiler with unroll should pipeline these. Actually even simpler and very effective: just unroll the load loop by a factor. The current loop: ``` for (int p = tid; p < len; p += BLOCK) { insert_topk(tv, ti, xrow[seg_start+p], seg_start+p); } ``` The load `xrow[seg_start+p]` is immediately consumed by insert. To pipeline, read several ahead. Let me unroll by 4-8 with explicit prefetch. But there's a subtlety: with `#pragma unroll N`, the compiler may already pipeline loads across iterations IF the insert doesn't depend on the next load... but insert depends on THIS load, and the next iteration's load is independent. The compiler should be able to overlap iteration i+1's load with iteration i's insert IF unrolled. Let me just add `#pragma unroll 8` to the streaming loop. But the loop trip count (len/BLOCK) is runtime, so unroll might not fully apply. Let me restructure to a known-trip-count inner loop. Let me restructure streaming into tiles: ``` int len = seg_end - seg_start; int p = threadIdx.x; // full tiles int full = (len / (BLOCK*UTILE)) * (BLOCK*UTILE); for (int base = 0; base < full; base += BLOCK*UTILE) { float vb[UTILE]; int ib[UTILE]; #pragma unroll for (int j=0;j<UTILE;j++){ vb[j]=xrow[seg_start+base + p + j*BLOCK]; ib[j]=seg_start+base+p+j*BLOCK; } #pragma unroll for (int j=0;j<UTILE;j++) insert_topk<K>(tv,ti,vb[j],ib[j]); } // tail for (int q = full + p; q < len; q += BLOCK) insert_topk<K>(tv,ti,xrow[seg_start+q],seg_start+q); ``` With UTILE=8, the 8 loads issue together (pipelined), hiding latency. This should dramatically speed up the read-bound shapes. Also, vectorized loads: use float4 to read 4 consecutive floats. But the access pattern is strided (thread t reads t, t+BLOCK...). For float4, thread t would read 4 consecutive elements [4t..4t+3], then next at [4t + 4*BLOCK]. That changes the mapping but is still valid (each element read once). Let me consider: with float4, the per-thread chunk = elements [4t, 4t+1, 4t+2, 4t+3, 4t+4B, ...]. Coalesced (warp reads 32*4=128 floats = 512 bytes contiguous). Good. And fewer load instructions. But combining float4 with the tiling prefetch: read float4 into 4 registers, insert each. Let me do float4 prefetch with UTILE float4s = 4*UTILE elements prefetched. Let me first just try the unrolled prefetch (scalar) with UTILE=8 and measure. If big win, then add float4. Actually, let me reconsider: is it really load-latency bound? The n-scaling: 8.5us per 4096 elements (per row, 256 threads → 16 elem/thread → 16 loads/thread). 16 loads * latency. If latency-hidden, should be ~0.5us (DRAM bw). 8.5us means ~17x slowdown → consistent with serial load-use with ~0 overlap (each load stalls). So prefetch should give a big win. Let me implement the tiled prefetch. Let me also keep it simple. Let me rewrite the streaming section of topk_reduce_kernel. Let me use UTILE such that we prefetch enough. With unroll, compiler issues all UTILE loads then the inserts. Let me set UTILE=8. Also for shape 0 (seg_size=2048, BLOCK=128 → 16 elem/thread), same fix applies. Let me edit the kernel.

Floor is ~11.5us; time scales linearly with n and K. The n-dependence (~8.5us per 4096 elems, ~17x above DRAM peak) means the streaming loop is **load-latency bound** — each thread does load→insert→load serially with no overlap. Fix: tile + prefetch (unrolled batch of loads issued before any insert) to hide DRAM latency.

+    // Tiled, prefetch-style streaming: issue UTILE loads up front (unrolled)
+    // before consuming any of them, so many global loads are in flight and DRAM
+    // latency is hidden. Without this the load->insert dependency serializes
+    // each thread to one outstanding load (~17x below DRAM peak).
+    constexpr int UTILE = 8;
+    const int len = seg_end - seg_start;
+    const int stride = BLOCK * UTILE;
+    int full = (len / stride) * stride;
+    for (int base = 0; base < full; base += stride) {
+        float vb[UTILE]; int ib[UTILE];
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int j = 0; j < UTILE; j++) {
+            int q = base + threadIdx.x + j * BLOCK;
+            vb[j] = xrow[seg_start + q];
+            ib[j] = seg_start + q;
+        }
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int j = 0; j < UTILE; j++) insert_topk<K>(tv, ti, vb[j], ib[j]);
+    }
+    for (int q = full + threadIdx.x; q < len; q += BLOCK) {
+        insert_topk<K>(tv, ti, xrow[seg_start + q], seg_start + q);
     }
 
     constexpr int M = BLOCK * (K + 1);

+// Fused big-row path: enqueue reduce + merge back-to-back in ONE host call so
+// there is no Python round-trip (and GPU idle gap) between the two launches.
+#define FUSED_BIG(KB, RBL, MBL) \
+extern "C" void launch_big_##KB(const float* x, float* ov, long long* oi, \
+    float* tv_, long long* ti_, int batch, int n, int nseg, int seg_size) { \
+    constexpr int RM = RBL * (KB + 1); \
+    size_t rsmem = (size_t)RM * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)); \
+    cudaFuncSetAttribute(topk_reduce_kernel<KB, RBL>, \
+        cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, 99 * 1024); \
+    dim3 grid(batch, nseg); \
+    topk_reduce_kernel<KB, RBL><<<grid, RBL, rsmem>>>( \
+        x, ov, oi, tv_, ti_, batch, n, nseg, seg_size); \
+    constexpr int MM = MBL * (KB + 1); \
+    size_t msmem = (size_t)MM * (sizeof(float) + sizeof(int)); \
+    cudaFuncSetAttribute(topk_merge_kernel<KB, MBL>, \
+        cudaFuncAttributeMaxDynamicSharedMemorySize, 99 * 1024); \
+    topk_merge_kernel<KB, MBL><<<batch, MBL, msmem>>>(tv_, ti_, ov, oi, nseg); \
+}
+FUSED_BIG(64, 128, 128)
 """

cat > prof4.py << 'EOF'
import torch, ctypes, statistics, solution
lib = solution._lib()
def ev(): return torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
def t(fn,it=200):
    for _ in range(30): fn()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    a=[]
    for _ in range(it):
        s,e=ev(),ev(); s.record(); fn(); e.record(); torch.cuda.synchronize(); a.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    return statistics.median(a)*1000

b,n,k=1,131072,64
seg_size=2048; nseg=64
x=torch.randn(b,n,device='cuda')
ov=torch.empty(b,k,device='cuda'); oi=torch.empty(b,k,device=torch.int64,device=None) if False else torch.empty(b,k,dtype=torch.int64,device='cuda')
tmpv=torch.empty(b,nseg,k,device='cuda'); tmpi=torch.empty(b,nseg,k,dtype=torch.int64,device='cuda')
xp=ctypes.c_void_p(x.data_ptr()); ovp=ctypes.c_void_p(ov.data_ptr()); oip=ctypes.c_void_p(oi.data_ptr())
tvp=ctypes.c_void_p(tmpv.data_ptr()); tip=ctypes.c_void_p(tmpi.data_ptr())
print(f"reduce_64 alone: {t(lambda: lib.launch_reduce_64(xp,ovp,oip,tvp,tip,b,n,nseg,seg_size)):.1f}us")
print(f"merge_64 alone:  {t(lambda: lib.launch_merge_64(tvp,tip,ovp,oip,b,nseg)):.1f}us")
print(f"big (fused):     {t(lambda: lib.launch_big_64(xp,ovp,oip,tvp,tip,b,n,nseg,seg_size)):.1f}us")
print(f"alloc only:      {t(lambda: torch.empty(b,nseg,k,device='cuda')):.1f}us")
# vary seg_size / nseg
for ss in [512,1024,2048,4096,8192]:
    ns=(n+ss-1)//ss; mp=1
    while mp<ns: mp<<=1
    ns=mp
    tmpv2=torch.empty(b,ns,k,device='cuda'); tmpi2=torch.empty(b,ns,k,dtype=torch.int64,device='cuda')
    tvp2=ctypes.c_void_p(tmpv2.data_ptr()); tip2=ctypes.c_void_p(tmpi2.data_ptr())
    print(f"  seg={ss} nseg={ns}: big={t(lambda: lib.launch_big_64(xp,ovp,oip,tvp2,tip2,b,n,ns,ss)):.1f}us")
EOF

import torch, ctypes, statistics, solution
lib = solution._lib()
def ev(): return torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
def t(fn,it=300):
    for _ in range(50): fn()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    a=[]
    for _ in range(it):
        s,e=ev(),ev(); s.record(); fn(); e.record(); torch.cuda.synchronize(); a.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    return statistics.median(a)*1000
# K=32 reduce (n=16384) for different batch (=> different #blocks/SMs used)
for b in [8,16,32,64,128]:
    n,k=16384,32
    x=torch.randn(b,n,device='cuda'); ov=torch.empty(b,k,device='cuda'); oi=torch.empty(b,k,dtype=torch.int64,device='cuda')
    xp=ctypes.c_void_p(x.data_ptr());ovp=ctypes.c_void_p(ov.data_ptr());oip=ctypes.c_void_p(oi.data_ptr())
    # per-row reduce nseg=1
    print(f"K=32 n=16384 batch={b} ({b} blocks): {t(lambda: lib.launch_reduce_32(xp,ovp,oip,xp,oip,b,n,1,n)):.1f}us")
# how does per-block time scale? batch=8 vs 128 -> if parallel, similar per-block
EOF

# build 3 variants of the K=32 reduce kernel: full / no-merge / no-insert-just-read
mkdir -p diag
cat > diag/build.py << 'EOF'
import subprocess, os
BASE = open("../solution.py").read()
# extract _CUDA_SRC
import re
m = re.search(r'_CUDA_SRC = r"""(.+?)"""', BASE, re.S)
src = m.group(1)
def build(name, extra_define):
    s = src.replace("#include <cuda_runtime.h>", "#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n#define "+extra_define) if extra_define else src
    # keep only the reduce_32 launch + a test main extern
    open(f"{name}.cu","w").write(s)
    subprocess.run(["nvcc","-O3","--use_fast_math","-std=c++17","-arch=sm_86","-Xcompiler=-fPIC","-ccbin","/usr/bin/g++-14","--shared",f"{name}.cu","-o",f"{name}.so"],check=True)
# variants
build("full","")
# no-merge: skip merge_tree (just dump, don't reduce) -> replace merge_tree call
src_nm = src.replace("merge_tree<BLOCK, K>(sv, si, BLOCK);","/*nomerge*/")
open("nomerge.cu","w").write(src_nm)
subprocess.run(["nvcc","-O3","--use_fast_math","-std=c++17","-arch=sm_86","-Xcompiler=-fPIC","-ccbin","/usr/bin/g++-14","--shared","nomerge.cu","-o","nomerge.so"],check=True)
# no-insert: replace insert call with just the load (no topk tracking)
src_ni = src.replace("insert_topk<K>(tv, ti, vb[j], ib[j]);","{ float v2=vb[j]; tv[0]=v2; ti[0]=ib[j]; }").replace("insert_topk<K>(tv, ti, xrow[seg_start + q], seg_start + q);","{ float v2=xrow[seg_start+q]; tv[0]=v2; ti[0]=seg_start+q; }")
open("noinsert.cu","w").write(src_ni)
subprocess.run(["nvcc","-O3","--use_fast_math","-std=c++17","-arch=sm_86","-Xcompiler=-fPIC","-ccbin","/usr/bin/g++-14","--shared","noinsert.cu","-o","noinsert.so"],check=True)
print("built")
EOF
cd diag && python build.py 2>&1 | tail -3
cat > timeit.py << 'EOF'
import ctypes, torch, statistics
def ev(): return torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
def t(fn,it=300):
    for _ in range(50): fn()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    a=[]
    for _ in range(it):
        s,e=ev(),ev(); s.record(); fn(); e.record(); torch.cuda.synchronize(); a.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    return statistics.median(a)*1000
b,n,k=32,16384,32
x=torch.randn(b,n,device='cuda'); ov=torch.empty(b,k,device='cuda'); oi=torch.empty(b,k,dtype=torch.int64,device='cuda')
xp=ctypes.c_void_p(x.data_ptr());ovp=ctypes.c_void_p(ov.data_ptr());oip=ctypes.c_void_p(oi.data_ptr())
for name in ["full","nomerge","noinsert"]:
    lib=ctypes.CDLL(f"{name}.so"); lib.launch_reduce_32.restype=None
    print(f"{name:10s}: {t(lambda: lib.launch_reduce_32(xp,ovp,oip,xp,oip,b,n,1,n)):.1f}us")
EOF

I thought most elements are below threshold (1 compare, return early). But nomerge = 33.6us suggests insert is expensive even for non-fill elements. Let me reconsider. Oh wait — the prefetch! Actually I bet the real issue is the unrolled cascade generates a LOT of instructions and the `active`/`carry` predicate chain is long. Let me reconsider the insert implementation: ``` if (v <= tv[K - 1]) return; float carry = v; int carry_i = gi; bool active = false; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { if (carry > tv[i]) active = true; if (active) { float tf = tv[i]; tv[i] = carry; carry = tf; int t2 = ti[i]; ti[i] = carry_i; carry_i = t2; } } ``` This is K=32 unrolled = 32 iterations. Each iteration: compare, conditional set active, conditional swap (4 reg moves). ~6 instructions * 32 = 192 instructions per insert-that-doesn't-return-early. For ~54 inserts/thread = 10k instructions/thread. 256 threads = 2.6M instructions/block. SM IPC ~ let's see. This is the cost. To reduce insert cost, I need fewer/cheaper inserts. But heap uses dynamic indexing (sift_down accesses heap[2*pos+1] with runtime pos) → register spill! Unless I keep heap in shmem. 2. **Keep heap in shared memory** with O(log K) insert. Shmem dynamic indexing is fine. 3. **Two-level: cheap pre-filter.** Each thread reads elements, compares to a coarse threshold (cheap), only inserts candidates above it. 4. **Reduce K-effective per thread** — but correctness needs K. Let me reconsider option: **keep the per-thread top-K in shared memory as a min-heap.** Insert = O(log K) shmem ops. For K=32: 5 levels. Much cheaper than O(K)=32 cascade. Better than 30us but not amazing. Alternatively, keep heap in registers and accept the sift-down via a small decision tree. Let me think if there's a register-friendly heap. Actually — the simplest register-friendly approach: **don't keep sorted; keep an unsorted register buffer + track the min index.** Insert: if v > min, replace buffer[min_idx] = v, then re-find min (scan K, O(K) but static unrolled, no dynamic store). Re-finding min is O(K) compares (static) but no shifts (no dynamic writes to a shifting array). ``` // buffer tv[K] unsorted, min stored separately if (v > min_val) { tv[min_idx] = v; // re-find min: scan all K, static loop, find argmin min_idx = 0; min_val = tv[0]; #pragma unroll for (int i=1;i<K;i++){ if(tv[i]<min_val){min_val=tv[i]; min_idx=i;} } } ``` Wait, but `min_idx = i` with runtime i assigning... and then `tv[min_idx]` with runtime min_idx → that's a dynamic STORE to tv[min_idx]. Does dynamic store to a register array cause spill? Dynamic READ causes spill; dynamic WRITE to register array also causes the compiler to spill (can't keep in regs with dynamic write). Hmm. Actually `tv[min_idx]=v` with runtime min_idx → spill. But the scan to find min: `min_val=tv[i]` with static i (unrolled) → reads tv[0..K-1] statically, no spill. The only dynamic index is the write `tv[min_idx]=v`. Hmm. To avoid the dynamic write, I could store the new value via a static cascade: write v to ALL positions conditionally (predicated): ``` #pragma unroll for (int i=0;i<K;i++){ if(i==min_idx) tv[i]=v; } ``` That's K predicated stores with static i → no spill. Cheap-ish (K compare+conditional). So insert = scan for new min (K compares) + K predicated stores = ~2K ops. Same O(K) as before, no better. OK the O(log K) heap is the way to actually reduce work. Let me just do the shmem heap and accept the shmem-latency cost (~5-6us), which is still 5x better than 30us. Actually, wait. Let me reconsider the magnitude. 30us for insert at K=32. Actually, let me reconsider the simplest possible thing: **increase UTILE and check if the insert is actually being optimized.** No, insert is fundamentally O(K) per qualifying element. Let me write a register bitonic sort for the K values+indices. Hmm, actually — simplest: after heap streaming, the buffer holds the K largest (unsorted). I load them into the tv[K] register array and run a compile-time bitonic sort to make tv sorted descending. Then dump to shmem merge layout. Let me write it. The register bitonic sort for arbitrary K (1,8,16,32,64) via templates: ``` template<int K> __device__ __forceinline__ void sort_desc(float* tv, int* ti) { // bitonic sort descending, fully unrolled, static indices for (int size=2; size<=K; size<<=1) { for (int stride=size>>1; stride>0; stride>>=1) { #pragma unroll for (int i=0;i<K;i++){ int j=i^stride; if (j>i) { bool up=((i&size)==0); if ((up && tv[i]<tv[j]) || (!up && tv[i]>tv[j])) { float f=tv[i];tv[i]=tv[j];tv[j]=f; int g=ti[i];ti[i]=ti[j];ti[j]=g; } } } } } } ``` All static indices (i,j computed from unrolled i and constexpr stride/size). No spill. Good. But for K=64 this is 64*6*... a lot of instructions but no spill and one-time. Now the heap. Insert: ``` __device__ void heap_insert(float* hv, int* hi, int base, float v, int gi, int K) { if (v <= hv[base]) return; hv[base]=v; hi[base]=gi; int pos=0; while (true) { int l=2*pos+1, r=2*pos+2, smallest=pos; if (l<K && hv[base+l]<hv[base+smallest]) smallest=l; if (r<K && hv[base+r]<hv[base+smallest]) smallest=r; if (smallest==pos) break; float f=hv[base+pos];hv[base+pos]=hv[base+smallest];hv[base+smallest]=f; int g=hi[base+pos];hi[base+pos]=hi[base+smallest];hi[base+smallest]=g; pos=smallest; } } ``` K runtime here (or template). +    if (v <= hv[base]) return;
+    hv[base] = v; hi[base] = gi;
+    int pos = 0;

+            float tf = tv[i]; tv[i] = carry; carry = tf;

+        }
     }

Else, the K-th largest has bit=0, subtract count from K (K-=count), restrict to bit=0 group. Wait, but we don't restrict the actual data — we track the threshold value being built. We count elements whose high bits match the threshold-so-far AND have the current bit set. Standard radix select. 3. +// -----------------------------------------------------------------------
+// Radix-select top-k (one block per row). 32 counting passes build the uint
+// value of the k-th largest; each pass re-reads the row, which is L2-resident
+// after the first pass (rows here are << 6 MB L2), so passes 2..32 run at L2
+// bandwidth -- much cheaper than an O(n*K) per-thread cascade for large K.
+// -----------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+// float -> monotonic uint (larger float -> larger uint). NaN maps to 0 (excluded).
+__device__ __forceinline__ unsigned f2u(float f) {
+    unsigned u = __float_as_int(f);
+    if ((u & 0x7f800000u) == 0x7f800000u && (u & 0x007fffffu)) return 0;  // NaN -> 0
+    return (u & 0x80000000u) ? ~u : (u | 0x80000000u);
+}
+
+template <int BLOCK>
+__device__ __forceinline__ int block_sum(int v, int tid) {
+    #pragma unroll
+    for (int d = 16; d > 0; d >>= 1) v += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffffu, v, d);
+    __shared__ int ws[BLOCK / 32];
+    int lane = tid & 31, wid = tid >> 5;
+    if (lane == 0) ws[wid] = v;
+    __syncthreads();
+    if (wid == 0) {
+        int x = (lane < BLOCK / 32) ? ws[lane] : 0;
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int d = (BLOCK / 32) / 2; d > 0; d >>= 1) x += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffffu, x, d);
+        if (lane == 0) ws[0] = x;
+    }
+    __syncthreads();
+    return ws[0];
+}
+
+template <int BLOCK, int K>
+__global__ void topk_radix_kernel(
+    const float* __restrict__ x, float* __restrict__ out_vals,
+    long long* __restrict__ out_idx, int n, int k)
+{
+    const int row = blockIdx.x;
+    const float* xr = x + (size_t)row * n;
+    const int tid = threadIdx.x;
+
+    __shared__ unsigned s_thr;
+
+    // --- find the k-th largest's monotonic uint (32 MSB-first bit passes) ---
+    unsigned threshold = 0;
+    for (int bit = 31; bit >= 0; bit--) {
+        unsigned candidate = threshold | (1u << bit);
+        int local = 0;
+        for (int p = tid; p < n; p += BLOCK) local += (f2u(xr[p]) >= candidate);
+        int total = block_sum<BLOCK>(local, tid);
+        if (tid == 0) s_thr = (total >= k) ? candidate : threshold;
+        __syncthreads();
+        threshold = s_thr;
+    }
+
+    // --- gather elements with M > threshold, then == threshold up to k ---
+    __shared__ float gsv[K];
+    __shared__ int gsi[K];
+    __shared__ int wpos;
+    if (tid == 0) wpos = 0;
+    __syncthreads();
+    for (int p = tid; p < n; p += BLOCK) {
+        unsigned u = f2u(xr[p]);
+        if (u > threshold) {
+            int pos = atomicAdd(&wpos, 1);
+            if (pos < k) { gsv[pos] = xr[p]; gsi[pos] = p; }
+        }
+    }
+    __syncthreads();
+    if (wpos < k) {
+        for (int p = tid; p < n; p += BLOCK) {
+            unsigned u = f2u(xr[p]);
+            if (u == threshold) {
+                int pos = atomicAdd(&wpos, 1);
+                if (pos < k) { gsv[pos] = xr[p]; gsi[pos] = p; }
+            }
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+    }
+
+    // --- sort the k gathered descending (thread 0, register bitonic) ---
+    if (tid == 0) {
+        float tv[K]; int ti[K];
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) { tv[i] = (i < k) ? gsv[i] : -INFINITY;
+                                       ti[i] = (i < k) ? gsi[i] : 0; }
+        sort_desc<K>(tv, ti);
+        #pragma unroll
+        for (int i = 0; i < K; i++) {
+            out_vals[(size_t)row * K + i] = tv[i];
+            out_idx [(size_t)row * K + i] = (long long)ti[i];
+        }
+    }
+}
+
+#define LAUNCH_RADIX(KB) \
+extern "C" void launch_radix_##KB(const float* x, float* ov, long long* oi, \
+    int batch, int n, int k) { \
+    topk_radix_kernel<1024, KB><<<batch, 1024>>>(x, ov, oi, n, k); \
+}
+LAUNCH_RADIX(8)
+LAUNCH_RADIX(16)
+LAUNCH_RADIX(32)
+LAUNCH_RADIX(64)
+

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor):
        b, n, k = self.batch, self.n, self.k
        x = x.contiguous()
        out_vals = torch.empty((b, k), dtype=torch.float32, device=x.device)
        out_idx = torch.empty((b, k), dtype=torch.int64, device=x.device)

        lib = _lib()
        xp = ctypes.c_void_p(x.data_ptr())
        ovp = ctypes.c_void_p(out_vals.data_ptr())
        oip = ctypes.c_void_p(out_idx.data_ptr())

        # K==1 is plain argmax -> cheap single-pass cascade. K>=8 uses radix
        # select (L2-resident multi-pass; far cheaper than O(n*K) cascade).
        if k == 1:
            getattr(lib, "launch_reduce_1")(xp, ovp, oip, xp, oip, b, n, 1, n)

import torch, ctypes, solution
lib = solution._lib()
for (b,n,k) in [(1,131072,64),(64,8192,8),(32,16384,32),(16,12000,16)]:
    x = torch.randn(b,n,device='cuda')
    ov = torch.empty(b,k,device='cuda'); oi = torch.empty(b,k,dtype=torch.int64,device='cuda')
    lib.__getattr__(f'launch_radix_{k}')(ctypes.c_void_p(x.data_ptr()),ctypes.c_void_p(ov.data_ptr()),ctypes.c_void_p(oi.data_ptr()),b,n,k)

import torch, ctypes, solution
lib = solution._lib()
def run(b,n,k):
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    x = torch.randn(b,n,device='cuda')
    ov = torch.empty(b,k,device='cuda'); oi = torch.empty(b,k,dtype=torch.int64,device='cuda')
    lib.__getattribute__(f'launch_radix_{k}')(ctypes.c_void_p(x.data_ptr()),ctypes.c_void_p(ov.data_ptr()),ctypes.c_void_p(oi.data_ptr()),b,n,k)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    rv,ri = torch.topk(x,k,sorted=True)
    print(f"b={b} n={n} k={k}: sol_max={ov.max().item():.4f} ref_max={rv.max().item():.4f} vals_match={torch.allclose(ov,rv,atol=1e-4)} idxrange=[{oi.min().item()},{oi.max().item()}]")
for k in [8,16,32,64]:
    run(2, 4096, k)   # small n, multiple blocks
